type=\"math/tex\">{3.6}_{-0.2}^{+0.2}</script></span></span>\n</td>\n</tr>\n</tbody></table><p><small>\n<p><strong>Notes.</strong></p>\n<a name=\"apjlaadc10t1fna\" id=\"apjlaadc10t1fna\"></a><sup> a</sup>We present 1<em>\u03c3</em> upper limits for undetected bands.\n<a name=\"apjlaadc10t1fnb\" id=\"apjlaadc10t1fnb\"></a><sup> b</sup>Photometric redshifts found using Bayesian photometric redshift (BPZ; Ben\u00edtez <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaadc10bib1a\" id=\"fnref-apjlaadc10bib1a\">2000</a>). The two <em>z</em>\u00a0~\u00a03 interlopers from PLCKG138-10 and RXC0018+16 were initially identified as <em>z</em>\u00a0~\u00a010 candidates prior to including the <em>Spitzer</em> data, whereas the candidate in SPT0615-57 remained at <em>z</em>\u00a0~\u00a010.\n</small></p><p class=\"franklin fs-16\">\n\t\t\tDownload table as:\u00a0\n            <span class=\"btn-multi-block\"><a class=\"btn btn-primary wd-jnl-art-btn-ascii\" href=\"/2041-8205/864/1/L22/suppdata/apjlaadc10t1_ascii.txt?doi=10.3847/2041-8213/aadc10\" target=\"_blank\">ASCII</a><a class=\"btn btn-primary wd-jnl-art-btn-typeset typeset-table-img\" href=\"/2041-8205/864/1/L22/suppdata/apjlaadc10t1_lr.gif\" target=\"_blank\">Typeset image</a></span></p></div></div><h3 id=\"apjlaadc10s2-2\" name=\"apjlaadc10s2-2\">2.2.\u00a0Spitzer Photometry</h3><div class=\"article-text\"><p>After finding the initial <em>z</em>\u00a0=\u00a09\u201310 candidates with <em>HST</em> data, we further vetted these candidates by checking <em>Spitzer</em> data from the S-RELICS programs (PI: Brada\u010d; PI: Soifer) including a recent Cycle 13 DDT program to observe SPT0615-57. The IRAC channel 1 and 2 bands (3.6 and 4.5 <em>\u03bc</em>m, respectively, with current depths per band of \u224817 hr), correspond to rest-frame optical flux at <em>z</em>\u00a0~\u00a09\u201310 and are invaluable for distinguishing between intrinsically bluer <em>z</em>\u00a0~\u00a010 star-forming galaxies and intrinsically redder <em>z</em>\u00a0~\u00a02 interloper galaxies. The <em>Spitzer</em> fluxes were extracted using <tt>T-PHOT</tt> (Merlin et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaadc10bib24\" id=\"fnref-apjlaadc10bib24\">2016</a>) which uses the higher resolution <em>HST</em> imaging as a prior to extract photometry from the lower resolution <em>Spitzer</em> images. First, we produce point-spread function (PSF) convolution kernels based on all available <em>HST</em> images. We manually sharpen the PSF to minimize residuals between the convolved image and <em>Spitzer</em> images. Then, we run <tt>T-PHOT</tt> on a localized region around the existing WFC3/IR imaging as opposed to the entire cluster field, which we have found produces cleaner residuals, indicating a more reliable source extraction. After obtaining the <em>Spitzer</em> photometry and recalculating photometric redshifts, we rule out two candidates as low-<em>z</em> interlopers, leaving SPT0615-JD1 as the only <em>z</em>\u00a0~\u00a010 candidate.</p><p>We note that Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjlaadc10f1\">1</a> also reveals an IR-bright nearby <em>z</em>\u00a0~\u00a03 galaxy. <tt>T-PHOT</tt> reports a high maximum covariance between the photometry of SPT0615-JD1 and its bright northwest neighbor. To test our ability to accurately determine <em>Spitzer</em> fluxes, we conducted an input/output photometry simulation. First, we used elliptical apertures to extract the images of JD1 and its bright neighbor from the F160W image. Next, we manually scaled their fluxes and convolved them with the <em>Spitzer</em> PSF. We then pasted each of these two new sources into a clear, nearby region of the real 3.6 <em>\u03bc</em>m image. We calculated the fluxes of the new sources in a blind experiment where the investigator running <tt>T-PHOT</tt> did not know the true input flux scalings. Finally, we compared the input and output fluxes and repeated the experiment 40 times for several input flux combinations and source locations.</p><p>We found that we are only able to accurately recover lensed magnitudes brighter than [3.6 <em>\u03bc</em>m]\u00a0\u2248\u00a024.5 mag for JD1, given its proximity to the brighter galaxy. For fainter simulated sources (including zero flux), our methods yield spurious magnitude measurements of \u224825 mag due to light from the neighboring galaxy. Given the relatively faint observed magnitude (Table <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"tabref\" href=\"#apjlaadc10t1\">1</a>, [3.6 <em>\u03bc</em>m]\u00a0=\u00a025.5\u00a0\u00b1\u00a00.4), we conclude that SPT0615-JD1 is not significantly brighter than its observed magnitude at 3.6 and 4.5 <em>\u03bc</em>m, but could be fainter.</p><p>Even assuming a conservatively higher magnitude (e.g., [3.6 <em>\u03bc</em>m]\u00a0=\u00a024), the H\u2212[3.6 <em>\u03bc</em>m] color is still several magnitudes lower than typical low-<em>z</em> interlopers. This is critical because while all <em>z</em>\u00a0~\u00a010 solutions could have lower <em>Spitzer</em> fluxes, the low-<em>z</em> solution <em>requires</em> them to be high. For a low-<em>z</em> spectral energy distribution (SED) to match the extreme red slope in the <em>HST</em> IR bands, it must have a high dust attenuation and therefore red F160W\u2212[3.6 <em>\u03bc</em>m] and [3.6 <em>\u03bc</em>m]\u2212[4.5 <em>\u03bc</em>m] colors, i.e., several magnitudes brighter in <em>Spitzer</em>. Conversely, the <em>Spitzer</em> bands sample rest-frame optical light for a high-<em>z</em> source, and the red slope in the <em>HST</em> bands is simply caused by the Lyman break. Our tests with BPZ show that the <em>z</em>\u00a0&lt;\u00a08 probability will only each &gt;5% when the lensing-corrected [3.6 <em>\u03bc</em>m] and [4.5 <em>\u03bc</em>m] magnitudes are &gt;24.5 mag (&gt;23 mag lensed), with the current <em>HST</em> photometry. Even adopting the upper-limit magnitude from our simulations (observed [3.6 <em>\u03bc</em>m]\u00a0\u2248\u00a024.5, and assuming the same red color as seen in the data of [3.6 <em>\u03bc</em>m]\u2212[4.5 <em>\u03bc</em>m]\u00a0=\u00a00.32 mag), and a low lensing magnification (<em>\u03bc</em>\u00a0=\u00a04, such that the object is intrinsically brighter, thus increasing low-<em>z</em> likelihood due to magnitude priors) the total <em>z</em>\u00a0&lt;\u00a08 probability is still <img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/Lt.gif\" alt=\"Lt\" align=\"absmiddle\" />1%.</p></div></div>\n<h2 class=\"header-anchor\" id=\"apjlaadc10s3\" name=\"apjlaadc10s3\">3.\u00a0Lens Models</h2><div class=\"article-text\" data-mobile-collapse=\"\"><p>Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjlaadc10f1\">1</a> shows three sets of multiply imaged galaxies: two with spectroscopic redshifts (<em>z</em>\u00a0=\u00a01.358 and <em>z</em>\u00a0=\u00a04.013) and one whose redshift is free to vary in the modeling. We use these sources to produce three independent lens models using <tt>Lenstool</tt> (see Paterno-Mahler et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaadc10bib30\" id=\"fnref-apjlaadc10bib30\">2018</a>, hereafter <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaadc10bib30\" id=\"fnref-apjlaadc10bib30\">PM18</a>), <tt>GLAFIC</tt> (Oguri <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaadc10bib28\" id=\"fnref-apjlaadc10bib28\">2010</a>), and the Zitrin et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaadc10bib45\" id=\"fnref-apjlaadc10bib45\">2015</a>) light-traces-mass (LTM) method. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaadc10bib30\" id=\"fnref-apjlaadc10bib30\">PM18</a> estimate the magnification of SPT0615-JD1 to be <em>\u03bc</em>\u00a0~\u00a04\u20137, and we adopt the upper end magnification throughout this work.</p><p>All three models predict two counter images at the positions shown in Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjlaadc10f1\">1</a>. Our results using <tt>GLAFIC</tt> (S. Kikuchihara et al. 2018, in preparation) and <tt>Lenstool</tt> (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaadc10bib30\" id=\"fnref-apjlaadc10bib30\">PM18</a>) predict that the northwest counter image is \u22481 mag fainter than the original arc, and therefore below the detection limit, or else potentially out of the field of view of the current WFC3/IR imaging. All three models predict the southeast counter image to be of similar magnification and magnitude of the original arc, and <tt>LTM</tt> predicts the counter images to have the same magnitude as the original arc. Given these models, we would have expected to see an image near the southeast position, but none are yet detected. Given the current shallow WFC3/IR depths (~26 mag in F160W), there still exist several explanations for the nondetection of the southeast counter image. For example, it would be below the detection limit if we assume the lowest end of the magnification estimates from <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaadc10bib30\" id=\"fnref-apjlaadc10bib30\">PM18</a> (<em>\u03bc</em>\u00a0=\u00a02.5). In addition, \"model 3\" from <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaadc10bib30\" id=\"fnref-apjlaadc10bib30\">PM18</a> (<em>\u03c7</em><sub><em>\u03bd</em></sub>\u00a0=\u00a00.96, which uses the less-secure arcs labeled blue in Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjlaadc10f1\">1</a>) does not predict a southeast counter image, but this is not the best-fitting model from their work. Conversely, all lens models predict no counter images if SPT0615-JD1 is at <em>z</em>\u00a0~\u00a02. Deeper imaging of this field is required to properly search for the <em>z</em>\u00a0~\u00a010 counter images and yield geometric support as in Coe et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaadc10bib12\" id=\"fnref-apjlaadc10bib12\">2013</a>), Zitrin et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaadc10bib46\" id=\"fnref-apjlaadc10bib46\">2014</a>), and Chan et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaadc10bib11\" id=\"fnref-apjlaadc10bib11\">2017</a>).</p></div>\n<h2 class=\"header-anchor\" id=\"apjlaadc10s4\" name=\"apjlaadc10s4\">4.\u00a0SED Fitting</h2><div class=\"article-text\" data-mobile-collapse=\"\"><p>Thanks to the <em>Spitzer</em> data that probes the rest-frame optical and near-UV (~2900\u20134500 \u00c5), we can infer upper limits on physical parameters like stellar mass and dust attenuation to test if the high- and low-<em>z</em> solutions are sensible. We use a Bayesian SED-fitting procedure originally described by Papovich et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaadc10bib29\" id=\"fnref-apjlaadc10bib29\">2001</a>) and updated by Salmon et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaadc10bib34\" id=\"fnref-apjlaadc10bib34\">2015</a>). In short, we sample the posterior using a grid of SEDs that represent a range of stellar population ages (10 Myr\u00a0&lt;\u00a0<em>t</em><sub>age</sub>\u00a0&lt; <em>t</em><sub>universe</sub>, logarithmically spaced), attenuation (0\u00a0&lt;\u00a0<em>A</em><sub>UV</sub>\u00a0&lt;\u00a07.4), metallicity (0.02 <em>Z</em><sub>\u2299</sub>\u00a0&lt;\u00a0<em>Z</em>\u00a0&lt;\u00a0<em>Z</em><sub>\u2299</sub>), and rising star formation histories (\u03a8(<em>t</em>)\u00a0=\u00a0\u03a8<sub>0</sub>exp(<em>t</em>/<em>\u03c4</em><sub>SFH</sub>), where the <em>e</em>-folding timescale <em>\u03c4</em><sub>SFH</sub> can be 0.3, 0.5, 0.7, 1, 3, 5, 7, 10, 30, 50, 70, or 100 Gyr). We use Bruzual &amp; Charlot (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaadc10bib7\" id=\"fnref-apjlaadc10bib7\">2003</a>) stellar population synthesis models with a Chabrier (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaadc10bib10\" id=\"fnref-apjlaadc10bib10\">2003</a>) IMF<sup xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"fnref\"><a class=\"fnref\" href=\"#apjlaadc10fn2\">2</a></sup>\n17 and include the effects of nebular emission lines following Salmon et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaadc10bib34\" id=\"fnref-apjlaadc10bib34\">2015</a>). We assume the dust-attenuation law derived by Salmon et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaadc10bib35\" id=\"fnref-apjlaadc10bib35\">2016</a>) that varies in shape from a steep law at low attenuation (similar in shape to the extinction law of the small magellanic cloud) to a gray law at high attenuation (similar in shape to the starburst curve of Calzetti et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaadc10bib9\" id=\"fnref-apjlaadc10bib9\">2000</a>)).</p><p>The results of our SED fitting are summarized in Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjlaadc10f2\">2</a>. For all SED fittings, we correct for lensing magnification assuming <em>\u03bc</em>\u00a0=\u00a07, and do not further correct the <em>Spitzer</em> fluxes despite likely contamination (see Section<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjlaadc10s2\"></a> 2 and Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjlaadc10f1\">1</a>). The fits assuming the <em>z</em>\u00a0~\u00a010 redshift show a moderately high stellar mass of <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/2041-8205/864/1/L22/Full/apjlaadc10ieqn11.gif\" alt=\"${M}_{\\star }={10}^{{8.3}_{-0.2}^{+0.3}}\\,{M}_{\\odot }$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">{M}_{\\star }={10}^{{8.3}_{-0.2}^{+0.3}}\\,{M}_{\\odot }</script></span></span> and SFR\u00a0=\u00a0<span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/2041-8205/864/1/L22/Full/apjlaadc10ieqn12.gif\" alt=\"${14}_{-8}^{+31}$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">{14}_{-8}^{+31}</script></span></span> <em>M</em><sub>\u2299</sub> yr<sup>\u22121</sup>. The stellar mass and SFR of SPT0615-JD1 are indicative of a typical star-forming galaxy at <em>z</em>\u00a0~\u00a010 (Oesch et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaadc10bib25\" id=\"fnref-apjlaadc10bib25\">2014</a>) and would lie on the SFR-<span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/2041-8205/864/1/L22/Full/apjlaadc10ieqn13.gif\" alt=\"${M}_{\\star }$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">{M}_{\\star }</script></span></span> relation at <em>z</em>\u00a0~\u00a06 (Salmon et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaadc10bib34\" id=\"fnref-apjlaadc10bib34\">2015</a>).</p><figure xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" id=\"apjlaadc10f2\" tabindex=\"-1\" role=\"group\" class=\"boxout boxout-bdr-grey keyboard-focus-only\" data-toolbar-type=\"figure\" data-toolbar-link=\"apjlaadc10f2\" data-toolbar-img=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/2041-8205/864/1/L22/Full/apjlaadc10f2_lr.jpg\" data-toolbar-title=\"Figure 2.\"><figure><div class=\"panzoom-container\"><div class=\"panzoom-parent\" style=\"overflow: hidden; position: relative;\"><img class=\"panzoom\" alt=\"Figure 2.\" src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/2041-8205/864/1/L22/Full/apjlaadc10f2_lr.jpg\" style=\"transform: matrix(1, 0, 0, 1, 0, 0); backface-visibility: hidden; transform-origin: 50% 50% 0px; cursor: move; transition: transform 200ms ease-in-out 0s;\"></img></div><div class=\"buttons zoom-tools\"><button class=\"zoom-in\"><span class=\"icon-zoomin\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom In\n\t\t\t\t</button><button class=\"zoom-out\"><span class=\"icon-zoomout\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom Out\n\t\t\t\t</button><span class=\"mobile-block\"><button class=\"reset\"><span class=\"icon-loop\"></span>\n\t                     Reset image size\n\t\t\t\t\t</button></span></div></div><figcaption><div class=\"article-text figure-caption\"><p><strong>Figure 2.</strong>\u00a0Best-fit SEDs to RELICS <em>HST</em> and <em>Spitzer</em> photometry (green circles) of SPT0615-JD1. The solid blue line and squares (dotted gray line and diamonds) show the best-fit SED and model fluxes, respectively, assuming the <em>z</em>\u00a0~\u00a010 (<em>z</em>\u00a0~\u00a02) solution. The top (bottom) plot displays the observed and lensing-corrected magnitudes (fluxes), the latter assuming a magnification of <em>\u03bc</em>\u00a0=\u00a07. Downward triangles show the <em>Spitzer</em> magnitude upper limits derived from photometry simulations (see Section <a class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjlaadc10s2\">2</a>). The top plot also shows the <em>HST</em> and <em>Spitzer</em> transmission curves for reference. Fluxes have been corrected for lensing magnification. The lower inset figure shows the redshift likelihood, <em>P</em>(<em>z</em>), which strongly favors the <em>z</em>\u00a0~\u00a010 solution, with &lt;1% likelihood for any <em>z</em>\u00a0&lt;\u00a06 solution.</p></div><p class=\"mb-05 print-hide\">Download figure:</p><span class=\"btn-multi-block print-hide\"><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-std-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-std-img-apjlaadc10f2\" href=\"/2041-8205/864/1/L22/downloadFigure/figure/apjlaadc10f2\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> Standard image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-hi-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-hires-img-apjlaadc10f2\" href=\"/2041-8205/864/1/L22/downloadHRFigure/figure/apjlaadc10f2\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> High-resolution image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a></span></figcaption></figure></figure><p>While the physical size of the candidate is large (see Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjlaadc10s5\">5</a>), its stellar mass and SFR indicate that it is a normal star-forming galaxy. The [3.6 <em>\u03bc</em>m]\u2212[4.5 <em>\u03bc</em>m] <em>Spitzer</em> color, which straddles the rest-frame 4000 \u00c5 break, is modestly red, but not enough to produce evidence of evolved stellar populations (aged \u2248300 Myr) like in other candidates (Hoag et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaadc10bib17\" id=\"fnref-apjlaadc10bib17\">2017</a>; Hashimoto et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaadc10bib16\" id=\"fnref-apjlaadc10bib16\">2018</a>).</p></div>\n<h2 class=\"header-anchor\" id=\"apjlaadc10s5\" name=\"apjlaadc10s5\">5.\u00a0Comparison with Other High-redshift Candidates</h2><div class=\"article-text\" data-mobile-collapse=\"\"><p>Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjlaadc10f3\">3</a> shows the <em>H</em>-band magnitude versus redshift for all high-<em>z</em> (<em>z</em>\u00a0&gt;\u00a05.5) candidate galaxies discovered in RELICS (Salmon et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaadc10bib33\" id=\"fnref-apjlaadc10bib33\">2017</a>) and many other deep and wide surveys. The lensed, observed-frame size of SPT0615-JD1 stands out as spatially much larger than other <em>z</em>\u00a0~\u00a010 candidates (other candidates at these redshifts have similar point-like sizes to those found by Coe et al<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaadc10bib12\" id=\"fnref-apjlaadc10bib12\"></a>. 2013 and Oesch et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaadc10bib27\" id=\"fnref-apjlaadc10bib27\">2016</a>, see below). The intrinsic (delensed) magnitude of SPT0615-JD1 is similar to that of the <em>z</em>\u00a0~\u00a011 candidate MACS0647-JD (Coe et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaadc10bib12\" id=\"fnref-apjlaadc10bib12\">2013</a>).</p><figure xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" id=\"apjlaadc10f3\" tabindex=\"-1\" role=\"group\" class=\"boxout boxout-bdr-grey keyboard-focus-only\" data-toolbar-type=\"figure\" data-toolbar-link=\"apjlaadc10f3\" data-toolbar-img=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/2041-8205/864/1/L22/Full/apjlaadc10f3_lr.jpg\" data-toolbar-title=\"Figure 3.\"><figure><div class=\"panzoom-container\"><div class=\"panzoom-parent\" style=\"overflow: hidden; position: relative;\"><img class=\"panzoom\" alt=\"Figure 3.\" src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/2041-8205/864/1/L22/Full/apjlaadc10f3_lr.jpg\" style=\"transform: matrix(1, 0, 0, 1, 0, 0); backface-visibility: hidden; transform-origin: 50% 50% 0px; cursor: move; transition: transform 200ms ease-in-out 0s;\"></img></div><div class=\"buttons zoom-tools\"><button class=\"zoom-in\"><span class=\"icon-zoomin\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom In\n\t\t\t\t</button><button class=\"zoom-out\"><span class=\"icon-zoomout\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom Out\n\t\t\t\t</button><span class=\"mobile-block\"><button class=\"reset\"><span class=\"icon-loop\"></span>\n\t                     Reset image size\n\t\t\t\t\t</button></span></div></div><figcaption><div class=\"article-text figure-caption\"><p><strong>Figure 3.</strong>\u00a0Observed <em>H</em>-band magnitude vs. redshift for <em>z</em>\u00a0&gt;\u00a05.5 candidates from various surveys. The <em>z</em>\u00a0&lt;\u00a08.5 candidates from Salmon et al. (<a class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaadc10bib33\" id=\"fnref-apjlaadc10bib33\">2017</a>) are shown as salmon-colored circles, and the candidates from SPT0615-57 are filled with crosses. The green squares are galaxies from CLASH (Zheng et al. <a class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaadc10bib44\" id=\"fnref-apjlaadc10bib44\">2012</a>; Bradley et al. <a class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaadc10bib6\" id=\"fnref-apjlaadc10bib6\">2014</a>; Hoag et al. <a class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaadc10bib17\" id=\"fnref-apjlaadc10bib17\">2017</a>), purple upwards triangles from the Frontier Field (Zitrin et al. <a class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaadc10bib46\" id=\"fnref-apjlaadc10bib46\">2014</a>; Ishigaki et al. <a class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaadc10bib20\" id=\"fnref-apjlaadc10bib20\">2018</a>), red downwards triangles from Ultra-VISTA (Bowler et al. <a class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaadc10bib4\" id=\"fnref-apjlaadc10bib4\">2017</a>), blue diamonds from CANDELS (Bouwens et al. <a class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaadc10bib2\" id=\"fnref-apjlaadc10bib2\">2014</a>, <a class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaadc10bib3\" id=\"fnref-apjlaadc10bib3\">2015</a>; Oesch et al. <a class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaadc10bib27\" id=\"fnref-apjlaadc10bib27\">2016</a>; outlined diamonds are from the HUDF; see also Finkelstein et al. <a class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaadc10bib15\" id=\"fnref-apjlaadc10bib15\">2015</a>), and orange pentagons from BoRG/HIPPIES (Bradley et al. <a class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaadc10bib5\" id=\"fnref-apjlaadc10bib5\">2012</a>; Schmidt et al. <a class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaadc10bib37\" id=\"fnref-apjlaadc10bib37\">2014</a>; Calvi et al. <a class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaadc10bib8\" id=\"fnref-apjlaadc10bib8\">2016</a>). Gray lines follow the conversion from apparent to absolute UV magnitude to reference for unlensed sources. 2''\u00a0<b>&#x00d7;</b>\u00a02'' cutout images of two <em>z</em>\u00a0~\u00a011 candidates (Coe et al. <a class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaadc10bib12\" id=\"fnref-apjlaadc10bib12\">2013</a>; Oesch et al. <a class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaadc10bib27\" id=\"fnref-apjlaadc10bib27\">2016</a>) and the <em>z</em>\u00a0~\u00a010 candidate from this work mark their observed magnitudes, respectively. For the latter two candidates, we also show their cutouts scaled in size according to their lens model to show an example of their intrinsic size (a full source-plane reconstruction of SPT0615-JD1 would show a smaller axis ratio). SPT0615-JD1 has a much larger observed size compared to the other candidates.</p></div><p class=\"mb-05 print-hide\">Download figure:</p><span class=\"btn-multi-block print-hide\"><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-std-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-std-img-apjlaadc10f3\" href=\"/2041-8205/864/1/L22/downloadFigure/figure/apjlaadc10f3\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> Standard image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-hi-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-hires-img-apjlaadc10f3\" href=\"/2041-8205/864/1/L22/downloadHRFigure/figure/apjlaadc10f3\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> High-resolution image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a></span></figcaption></figure></figure><p>An independent way to test high- and low-<em>z</em> solutions for SPT0615-JD1 is to calculate its physical size and compare to other known interlopers. Moreover, the sizes of galaxies can give us great physical insight into the initial conditions of early disk evolution (Ferguson et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaadc10bib13\" id=\"fnref-apjlaadc10bib13\">2004</a>). Broadly, the <em>z</em>\u00a0&gt;\u00a05 size evolution at fixed luminosity scales as (1\u00a0+\u00a0<em>z</em>)<sup>\u2212<em>m</em></sup> where <em>m</em>\u00a0=\u00a01\u20132 (Shibuya et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaadc10bib38\" id=\"fnref-apjlaadc10bib38\">2015</a>). Holwerda et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaadc10bib18\" id=\"fnref-apjlaadc10bib18\">2015</a>) demonstrated that a combination of UV-to-optical color, sampled by the F160W and 3.6 <em>\u03bc</em>m bands, and physical size can be used to identify obvious low-<em>z</em> contaminants. They summarized that the sizes of <em>z</em>\u00a0&gt;\u00a09 galaxy candidates have typical half-light radii of <em>r</em><sub><em>e</em></sub>\u00a0&lt;\u00a00.8 kpc.</p><p>To calculate the size of SPT0615-JD1, we used our lens models to reconstruct its image in the source plane. The LTM lens model finds a relatively mild tangential magnification, or shear, of a factor of ~3, leaving the full width of the delensed source to be about 3\u20133.5 kpc. If we assume that the light distribution is uniform, we can take the half-light radius to be about ~1/4 of the full size and find <em>r</em><sub><em>e</em></sub>\u00a0\u2248\u00a00.7\u20130.8 kpc. The statistical error on this size (from the lens model) is only a couple of percent, so we are dominated by systematic errors (~10%). Curiously, the reconstructed source's axis ratio is still about 2:1 in the same direction as the lensing shear, which could mean that the shear is underestimated and the size is in fact smaller.</p><p>Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjlaadc10f4\">4</a> shows that the inferred size of SPT0615-JD1 is typical compared to other high-<em>z</em> candidates. This provides crucial evidence in support of the <em>z</em>\u00a0~\u00a010 solution that is independent of the galaxy SED. While the uncertainty in the <em>z</em>\u00a0~\u00a010 UV dust attenuation should be considered as an upper limit, the candidate is still within the range of <em>M</em><sub>UV</sub> and SFR surface density of known <em>z</em>\u00a0&gt;\u00a09 candidates.</p><figure xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" id=\"apjlaadc10f4\" tabindex=\"-1\" role=\"group\" class=\"boxout boxout-bdr-grey keyboard-focus-only\" data-toolbar-type=\"figure\" data-toolbar-link=\"apjlaadc10f4\" data-toolbar-img=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/2041-8205/864/1/L22/Full/apjlaadc10f4_lr.jpg\" data-toolbar-title=\"Figure 4.\"><figure><div class=\"panzoom-container\"><div class=\"panzoom-parent\" style=\"overflow: hidden; position: relative;\"><img class=\"panzoom\" alt=\"Figure 4.\" src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/2041-8205/864/1/L22/Full/apjlaadc10f4_lr.jpg\" style=\"transform: matrix(1, 0, 0, 1, 0, 0); backface-visibility: hidden; transform-origin: 50% 50% 0px; cursor: move; transition: transform 200ms ease-in-out 0s;\"></img></div><div class=\"buttons zoom-tools\"><button class=\"zoom-in\"><span class=\"icon-zoomin\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom In\n\t\t\t\t</button><button class=\"zoom-out\"><span class=\"icon-zoomout\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom Out\n\t\t\t\t</button><span class=\"mobile-block\"><button class=\"reset\"><span class=\"icon-loop\"></span>\n\t                     Reset image size\n\t\t\t\t\t</button></span></div></div><figcaption><div class=\"article-text figure-caption\"><p><strong>Figure 4.</strong>\u00a0Size of SPT0615-JD1 compared to other known <em>z</em>\u00a0&gt;\u00a09 galaxy candidates (blue circles). Left: the physical size (effective radius) as a function of absolute UV magnitude. The delensed magnitude is shown as the red circle and star, and the unobscured (corrected for our upper-limit dust extinction) delensed magnitude is shown as the salmon-colored star. Right: the physical size as a function of [F160W\u22123.6 <em>\u03bc</em>m] color. Triangles show <em>z</em>\u00a0&gt;\u00a09 candidates, including SPT0615-JD1 (bold triangle), that may be contaminated in <em>Spitzer</em> flux by a bright neighbor, resulting in redder [F160W\u22123.6 <em>\u03bc</em>m] colors. Significantly larger sizes of typical very red <em>z</em>\u00a0~\u00a02 galaxies (red squares) are shown in the red box.</p></div><p class=\"mb-05 print-hide\">Download figure:</p><span class=\"btn-multi-block print-hide\"><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-std-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-std-img-apjlaadc10f4\" href=\"/2041-8205/864/1/L22/downloadFigure/figure/apjlaadc10f4\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> Standard image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-hi-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-hires-img-apjlaadc10f4\" href=\"/2041-8205/864/1/L22/downloadHRFigure/figure/apjlaadc10f4\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> High-resolution image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a></span></figcaption></figure></figure></div>\n<h2 class=\"header-anchor\" id=\"apjlaadc10s6\" name=\"apjlaadc10s6\">6.\u00a0Conclusions</h2><div class=\"article-text\" data-mobile-collapse=\"\"><p>We present SPT0615-JD1, a promising <em>z</em>\u00a0~\u00a010 galaxy candidate that appears to be stretched into the shape of an arc by the effects of strong gravitational lensing. Out of all combined lensing fields from RELICS, CLASH, and the Frontier Fields, there is no other galaxy candidate spatially stretched by lensing as distant as SPT0615-JD1. While our three independent lens models predict at least one detectable counter image, we do not see one in the current data. No counter images are expected if the candidate is at lower redshift. After deriving photometry from our deep <em>Spitzer</em> imaging, and validating our conclusions with recovery simulations, we conclude that SPT0615-JD1 is a typical star-forming galaxy at <em>z</em>\u00a0~\u00a010, with &lt;1% likelihood for lower redshift solutions. Finally, we find that the source-plane size of SPT0615-JD1 is large, but comparable to other <em>z</em>\u00a0=\u00a09\u201310 galaxies, while the observed-frame image offers unprecedented spatial resolution. This large size could be due to a merger or accretion event, although higher resolution imaging is required to investigate further. This galaxy candidate offers the unique opportunity for resolving stellar populations deep in the epoch of reionization, especially with the greater sensitivity and higher resolution imaging of <em>JWST</em>.</p></div> <div class=\"article-text\" data-mobile-collapse=\"\"><p>This Letter uses observations from NASA/ESA <em>HST</em>. STScI is operated by AURA under NASA contract NAS 5-26555, ACS under NASA contract NAS 5-32864, and <em>Spitzer</em> by JPL. These observations are associated with program GO-14096 and archival data are associated with programs GO-12757 and GO-12477. Some data were obtained from MAST. The RELICS archive at MAST can be obtained at doi:<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"dataset\" target=\"_blank\" href=\"https://doi.org/10.17909/T9SP45\" data-art-data-content-type=\"External repository\" data-toolbar-link=\"https://doi.org/10.17909/T9SP45\" data-toolbar-title=\"MAST dataset\" data-art-data-type=\"ext-rep-mast\" id=\"https://doi.org/10.17909/T9SP45\">10.17909/T9SP45</a>. This work was performed under the auspices of the U.S. Department of Energy by LLNL under contract DE-AC52-07NA27344. F.A.-S. acknowledges support from Chandra grant G03-14131X.</p></div></div><!-- End article full text -->\n<!-- Start Footnotes -->\n\t<h2 id=\"footnotes\">Footnotes</h2>\n\t\t<div The few z \u223c 10-11 candidates discovered to date have been exceptionally small, barely resolved, if at all, by the Hubble Space Telescope. Here we present the discovery of SPT0615-JD1, a fortuitous z \u223c 10 (z <SUB>phot</SUB> = {9.9}<SUB>-0.6</SUB><SUP>+0.8</SUP>) galaxy candidate stretched into an arc over \u223c2.\u20335 by the effects of strong gravitational lensing. Discovered in the Reionization Lensing Cluster Survey (RELICS) Hubble Treasury program and companion S-RELICS Spitzer program, this candidate has a lensed H-band magnitude of 24.6 \u00b1 0.1 AB mag. With a magnification of \u03bc \u223c 4-7 estimated from our lens models, the delensed intrinsic magnitude is 26.7 \u00b1 0.1 AB mag, and the half-light radius is r <SUB> e </SUB> &lt; 0.8 kpc, both consistent with other z &gt; 9 candidates. The inferred stellar mass ({log}[{M}<SUB>\\star </SUB>/{M}<SUB>\u2609 </SUB>]={8.3}<SUB>-0.2</SUB><SUP>+0.3</SUP>) and star formation rate ({log}[{SFR}/{M}<SUB>\u2609 </SUB> {yr}}<SUP>-1</SUP>]={1.1}<SUB>-0.4</SUB><SUP>+0.5</SUP>) indicate that this candidate is a typical star-forming galaxy on the z &gt; 6 SFR-M <SUB>\u22c6</SUB> relation. We note that three independent lens models predict two counter images, at least one of which should be of a similar magnitude to the arc, but these counter images are not yet detected. Counter images would not be expected if the arc were at lower redshift. The relatively large physical size could be due to a merger or accretion event, while the unprecedented lensed size of this z \u223c 10 candidate offers the potential for ALMA and the James Webb Space Telescope to study the geometric and kinematic properties of a galaxy observed 500 Myr after the Big Bang.", "start_page": "L22", "authors": ["Salmon, Brett", "Coe, Dan", "Bradley, Larry", "Brada\u010d, Marusa", "Strait, Victoria", "Paterno-Mahler, Rachel", "Huang, Kuang-Han", "Oesch, Pascal A.", "Zitrin, Adi", "Acebron, Ana", "Cibirka, Nath\u00e1lia", "Kikuchihara, Shotaro", "Oguri, Masamune", "Brammer, Gabriel B.", "Sharon, Keren", "Trenti, Michele", "Avila, Roberto J.", "Ogaz, Sara", "Andrade-Santos, Felipe", "Carrasco, Daniela", "Cerny, Catherine", "Dawson, William", "Frye, Brenda L.", "Hoag, Austin", "Jones, Christine", "Mainali, Ramesh", "Ouchi, Masami", "Rodney, Steven A.", "Stark, Daniel", "Umetsu, Keiichi"], "issn": "0004-637X", "custom1": "eprint: arXiv:1801.03103", "Title": "RELICS: A Candidate z \u223c 10 Galaxy Strongly Lensed into a Spatially Resolved Arc", "type_of_reference": "JOUR", "keywords": ["galaxies: clusters: general", "galaxies: evolution", "galaxies: high-redshift", "gravitational lensing: strong", "Astrophysics - <div class=\"overlay-text\">\n            <p>The American Astronomical Society (AAS), established in 1899 and based in Washington, DC, is the major organization of professional astronomers in North America. Its membership of about 7,000 individuals also includes physicists, mathematicians, geologists, engineers, and others whose research and educational interests lie within the broad spectrum of subjects comprising contemporary astronomy. The mission of the AAS is to enhance and share humanity's scientific understanding of the universe.</p> <p><a href=\"https://aas.org/\">https://aas.org/</a></div>\n        <p class=\"ta-c mt-1 mb-2\"><a href=\"\"></a></p>\n    </div>\n</span>\n<!-- Partner logo tile ends -->\n\n<!-- Partner logo tile starts -->\n<a href=\"\" class=\"overlay-launch\">\n    <img border=\"0\" src=\"https://cms.iopscience.org/a56c0f0e-0439-11e9-b401-cfe9679c40e3/iop-2016.png?guest=true\" alt=\"The Institute of Physics, find out more\">\n</a>\n<span class=\"overlay-set\">\n    <div class=\"tint-screen\"></div>\n    <div class=\"overlay-panel\">\n        <a title=\"Close\"  class=\"close-icon close-overlay\" tabindex=\"0\" role=\"button\" aria-describedby=\"close-icon-description\"><span class=\"icon-close\"></span></a>\n        <p id=\"close-icon-description\" class=\"offscreen-hidden\">Click here to close this overlay, or press the \"Escape\" key on your keyboard.</p>\n        <div class=\"overlay-img\">\n            <a href=\"\">\n                <img border=\"0\" src=\"https://cms.iopscience.org/a56c0f0e-0439-11e9-b401-cfe9679c40e3/iop-2016.png?guest=true\" alt=\"The Institute of Physics, find out more\">\n            </a>\n        </div>\n        <div class=\"overlay-text\">\n            <p>The Institute of Physics (IOP) is a leading scientific society promoting physics and bringing physicists together for the benefit of all. It has a worldwide membership of around 50 000 comprising physicists from all sectors, as well as those with an interest in physics. It works to advance physics research, application and education; and engages with policy makers and the public to develop awareness and understanding of physics. Its publishing company, IOP Publishing, is a world leader in professional scientific communications.</p> <p><a href=\"https://www.iop.org\">https://www.iop.org</a></p></div>\n        <p class=\"ta-c End Email Alert -->\n<!-- End Email Alert --><!-- Start Citation Alert Link -->\n                        <p>\n<div class=\"jnl-notifications-wrapper\">\n    <a class=\"wd-jnl-email-alert\" -->\n<!-- Start Info top-->\n    <!-- end Info top --><div class=\"article-content\">\n\t<span id=\"articleId\" class=\"hide\">2041-8205/864/1/L22</span>\n<!-- Start Abstract -->\n    <h2 id=\"artAbst\" class=\"collapse-blocked\">Abstract</h2>\n        <!-- Start article video abstract -->\n\t<!-- End article video abstract -->\n\t<div class=\"article-text wd-jnl-art-abstract cf\" itemprop=\"description\">\n             <P>The most distant galaxies known are at <I>z</I>\u00a0~\u00a010\u201311, observed 400\u2013500 Myr after the Big Bang. The few <I>z</I>\u00a0~\u00a010\u201311 candidates discovered to date have been exceptionally small, barely resolved, if at all, by the <I>Hubble</I>\u00a0<I>Space</I>\u00a0<I>Telescope</I>. Here we present the discovery of SPT0615-JD1, a fortuitous <I>z</I>\u00a0~\u00a010 (<I>z</I> <SUB>phot</SUB>\u00a0=\u00a0<IMG ALIGN=\"MIDDLE\" alt=\"${9.9}_{-0.6}^{+0.8}$\" src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/2041-8205/864/1/L22/apjlaadc10ieqn1.gif\"></IMG>) galaxy candidate stretched into an arc over ~2<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/farcs.gif\" alt=\"farcs\" align=\"bottom\" />5 by the effects of strong gravitational lensing. Discovered in the Reionization Lensing Cluster Survey (RELICS) <I>Hubble</I> Treasury program and companion S-RELICS <I>Spitzer</I> program, this candidate has a lensed <I>H</I>-band magnitude of 24.6\u00a0\u00b1\u00a00.1 AB mag. With a magnification of <I>\u03bc</I>\u00a0~\u00a04\u20137 estimated from our lens models, the delensed intrinsic magnitude is 26.7\u00a0\u00b1\u00a00.1 AB mag, and the half-light radius is <I>r</I> <SUB> <I>e</I> </SUB>\u00a0&lt;\u00a00.8 kpc, both consistent with other <I>z</I>\u00a0&gt;\u00a09 candidates. The inferred stellar mass (<IMG ALIGN=\"MIDDLE\" alt=\"$\\mathrm{log}[{M}_{\\star }/{M}_{\\odot }]={8.3}_{-0.2}^{+0.3}$\" src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/2041-8205/864/1/L22/apjlaadc10ieqn2.gif\"></IMG>) and star formation rate (<IMG ALIGN=\"MIDDLE\" alt=\"$\\mathrm{log}[\\mathrm{SFR}/{M}_{\\odot }\\,{\\mathrm{yr}}^{-1}]={1.1}_{-0.4}^{+0.5}$\" src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/2041-8205/864/1/L22/apjlaadc10ieqn3.gif\"></IMG>) indicate that this candidate is a typical star-forming galaxy on the <I>z</I>\u00a0&gt;\u00a06 SFR\u2013<I>M</I> <SUB><img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/sstarf.gif\" alt=\"sstarf\" align=\"baseline\" /></SUB> relation. We note that three independent lens models predict two counter images, at least one of which should be of a similar magnitude to the arc, but these counter images are not yet detected. Counter images would not be expected if the arc were at lower redshift. The relatively large physical size could be due to a merger or accretion event, while the unprecedented lensed size of this <I>z</I>\u00a0~\u00a010 candidate offers the potential for <I>ALMA</I> and the <I>James</I>\u00a0<I>Webb</I>\u00a0<I>Space</I>\u00a0<I>Telescope</I> to study the geometric and kinematic properties of a galaxy observed 500 Myr after the Big Bang.</P> </div>\n    <!-- End abstract -->\n<!-- Start Export citation and abstract tools -->\n\t<p>\n\t\t<small>Export citation and abstract</small>\n\t\t<span class=\"btn-multi-block\">\n\t\t\t<a title=\"Export BibTex\">BibTeX</a>\n\t\t\t<a href=\"/export?articleId=2041-8205/864/1/L22&doi=10.3847/2041-8213/aadc10&exportFormat=iopexport_ris&exportType=abs&navsubmit=Export+abstract\" class=\"btn btn-primary wd-btn-cit-abs-ris\" title=\"Export RIS\">RIS</a>\n\t\t</span>\n\t</p>\n<!-- End Export citation and abstract tools -->\n<!-- Start AAS Related Links -->\n<div class=\"reveal-container reveal-closed reveal-plus-icon bdt-1 mt-2 mb-2 wd-jnl-art-article-aas-related-links\">\n  <div class=\"replica-h3 mt-0\">\n    <a href=\"#\" class=\"reveal-trigger\" data-reveal-label-alt=\"Hide related links\">Related links</a>\n  </div>\n  <div class=\"reveal-content\">\n    <div class=\"article-meta\"> \n      <ul>\n      \t<li><a href=\"http://adsabs.harvard.edu/abs/2018ApJ...864L..22S\" href=\"http://simbad.u-strasbg.fr/simbad/sim-ref?querymethod=bib&simbo=on&bibcode=2018ApJ...864L..22S\" target=\"_blank\">Simbad Objects <span class=\"icon-newtab\"></span></a></li>\n      \t<li><a href=\"/journal/2041-8205/page/About%20Related%20Links\" target=\"_blank\">About Related Links</a></li>\n      \t</ul>\n    </div>\n  </div>\n</div>\n<!-- Start info bottom -->\n    <!-- End info bottom -->\n<!-- Start article full text -->\n\t<div xmlns:book=\"http://api.iop.org/Book/1.0/\" itemprop=\"articleBody\" class=\"wd-jnl-art-full-text article-text\">\n<h2 class=\"header-anchor\" id=\"apjlaadc10s1\" name=\"apjlaadc10s1\">1.\u00a0Introduction</h2><div class=\"article-text\" data-mobile-collapse=\"\"><p>With its high resolution and sensitivity, observations using the <em>Hubble Space Telescope</em> (<em>HST</em>) have sharpened our understanding of the high-<em>z</em> universe. Deep and wide extragalactic imaging surveys with ACS and WFC3 have uncovered thousands of galaxies at <em>z</em>\u00a0&gt;\u00a06 in blank fields (see Finkelstein <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaadc10bib14\" id=\"fnref-apjlaadc10bib14\">2016</a>; Stark <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaadc10bib40\" id=\"fnref-apjlaadc10bib40\">2016</a>, for reviews), including the most distant galaxy found to date at <em>z</em>\u00a0=\u00a011.1 (GN-z11; Oesch et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaadc10bib27\" id=\"fnref-apjlaadc10bib27\">2016</a>). In addition, we have prioritized the <em>HST</em> to observe the most massive galaxy clusters, taking advantage of the natural telescopes they create via strong gravitational lensing (CLASH, PI: Postman; Frontier Fields, PI: Lotz; RELICS, PI: Coe). This investment in lensing fields has proven fruitful. We have discovered highly magnified (MACS1149-JD, Zheng et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaadc10bib44\" id=\"fnref-apjlaadc10bib44\">2012</a>; Hoag et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaadc10bib17\" id=\"fnref-apjlaadc10bib17\">2017</a>; MACS1115-JD and MACS1720-JD, Bouwens et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaadc10bib2\" id=\"fnref-apjlaadc10bib2\">2014</a>; MACS0416-JD, Infante et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaadc10bib19\" id=\"fnref-apjlaadc10bib19\">2015</a>) and multiply imaged galaxies (MACS0647-JD, Coe et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaadc10bib12\" id=\"fnref-apjlaadc10bib12\">2013</a>; A2744-JD, Zitrin et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaadc10bib46\" id=\"fnref-apjlaadc10bib46\">2014</a>) at redshifts up to <em>z</em>\u00a0~\u00a010.8, which have allowed us to study faint UV metal lines (Stark et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaadc10bib41\" id=\"fnref-apjlaadc10bib41\">2014</a>; Rigby et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaadc10bib32\" id=\"fnref-apjlaadc10bib32\">2015</a>; Mainali et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaadc10bib23\" id=\"fnref-apjlaadc10bib23\">2017</a>), nebular emission lines (Stark et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaadc10bib42\" id=\"fnref-apjlaadc10bib42\">2015</a>; Laporte et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaadc10bib22\" id=\"fnref-apjlaadc10bib22\">2017</a>; Smit et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaadc10bib39\" id=\"fnref-apjlaadc10bib39\">2017</a>), and the star formation rate (SFR) density deep into the epoch of reionization (Oesch et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaadc10bib25\" id=\"fnref-apjlaadc10bib25\">2014</a>, <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaadc10bib26\" id=\"fnref-apjlaadc10bib26\">2018</a>).</p><p>However, little is known in detail about the <em>z</em>\u00a0&gt;\u00a09 universe, and the handful of candidates found so far exhibit peculiar properties. At <em>z</em>\u00a0~\u00a011, MACS0647-JD has a radius smaller than 100 pc, the size of giant molecular clouds in the local universe. GN-z11 is three times brighter than the characteristic UV luminosity (<em>L</em><sub>*</sub>) of galaxies at that distance, surprisingly bright given the CANDELS search area. Both <em>z</em>\u00a0~\u00a0 9 and ~10 candidates MACS1149-JD and MACS0416-JD (the former spectroscopically confirmed; Hashimoto et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaadc10bib16\" id=\"fnref-apjlaadc10bib16\">2018</a>) appear to have evolved stellar populations of \u2248300 Myr, when the age of the universe was only \u2248500 Myr. <em>JWST</em> NIRCam will better sample the rest-frame UV-to-optical colors which will break some parameter degeneracies and challenge these initial inferences. However, with typical <em>z</em>\u00a0~\u00a010 effective radii of &lt;0<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/farcs.gif\" alt=\"farcs\" align=\"bottom\" />2 and a NIRCAM PSF FWHM<sup xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"fnref\"><a class=\"fnref\" href=\"#apjlaadc10fn1\">16</a></sup>\n of ~0<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/farcs.gif\" alt=\"farcs\" align=\"bottom\" />05 at 1.5 <em>\u03bc</em>m, it will still be difficult resolve these galaxies spatially. Ideally, we can use the help of strong lensing to study the kinematics and intrinsic stellar populations at <em>z</em>\u00a0~\u00a010 in detail.</p><p>In this Letter we present a galaxy gravitationally lensed into an arc with a photometric redshift of <em>z</em><sub>phot</sub>\u00a0=\u00a09.9\u00a0\u00b1\u00a00.6. Discovered in the Reionization Lensing Cluster Survey (RELICS) <em>Hubble</em><em> </em>(<em>HST</em>) and <em>Spitzer</em> <em>Space Telescope </em>imaging, the arc features of this candidate extend across ~2<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/farcs.gif\" alt=\"farcs\" align=\"bottom\" />5, allowing unprecedented physical resolution deep in the epoch of reionization. This new candidate has an <em>HST</em> F160W <em>H</em>-band magnitude of <em>H</em>\u00a0=\u00a024.6\u00a0\u00b1\u00a00.1 AB, bright enough for follow-up spectroscopic or grism observations. In this work, we present the physical characteristics of this candidate and discuss the supporting evidence of its high redshift. Throughout, we assume concordance cosmology with <em>H</em><sub>0</sub>\u00a0=\u00a070 km s<sup>\u22121</sup> Mpc<sup>\u22121</sup>, \u03a9<sub>\u039b,0</sub>\u00a0=\u00a00.7, and \u03a9<sub>M,0</sub>\u00a0=\u00a00.3.</p></div>\n<h2 class=\"header-anchor\" id=\"apjlaadc10s2\" name=\"apjlaadc10s2\">2.\u00a0Data and Photometry</h2><div class=\"article-text\" data-mobile-collapse=\"\"><h3 id=\"apjlaadc10s2-1\" name=\"apjlaadc10s2-1\">2.1.\u00a0Cluster Field and <em>HST</em> Photometry</h3><div class=\"article-text\"><p>The galaxy cluster SPT-CL J0615-5746 (hereafter SPT0615-57; also known as PLCK G266.6-27.3) was discovered independently by the South Pole Telescope survey (SPT; Williamson et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaadc10bib43\" id=\"fnref-apjlaadc10bib43\">2011</a>) and Planck Collaboration et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaadc10bib31\" id=\"fnref-apjlaadc10bib31\">2011</a>). It is exceptionally massive (<span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/2041-8205/864/1/L22/Full/apjlaadc10ieqn4.gif\" alt=\"${M}_{500}=7.1\\times {10}^{14}\\,{M}_{\\odot }$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">{M}_{500}=7.1\\times {10}^{14}\\,{M}_{\\odot }</script></span></span>) for its high redshift (<em>z</em>\u00a0=\u00a00.972). The SPT and Planck teams obtained <em>HST</em> imaging (GO 12477 and 12757) of the cluster with the ACS/WFC F606W filter <em>V</em> (one-orbit depth) and F814W filter <em>I</em> (combined two-orbit depth). RELICS (GO 14096) obtained ACS/WFC imaging (1 orbit) in F435W <em>B</em> and WFC3/IR imaging (two orbits) in F105W <em>Y</em>, F125W <em>J</em>, F140W <em>JH</em>, and F160W <em>H</em>.</p><p>RELICS obtained similar <em>HST</em> imaging with WFC3/IR and ACS on a total of 41 clusters. The details of the image reduction, SExtractor (version 2.8.6; Bertin &amp; Arnouts <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaadc10bib1\" id=\"fnref-apjlaadc10bib1\">1996</a>) object selection, and <em>HST</em> photometry are described by Salmon et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaadc10bib33\" id=\"fnref-apjlaadc10bib33\">2017</a>) and D. Coe et al. (2018, in preparation). SPT0615-57 is the highest redshift cluster and the second-highest producer of high-<em>z</em> candidates out of the 41 RELICS fields, revealing 25 new candidate galaxies over the redshift range 5.5\u00a0&lt;\u00a0<em>z</em>\u00a0&lt;\u00a08.5 (Salmon et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaadc10bib33\" id=\"fnref-apjlaadc10bib33\">2017</a>).</p><p>Table <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"tabref\" href=\"#apjlaadc10t1\">1</a> shows the three <em>z</em>\u00a0&gt;\u00a09 candidates found in RELICS after fitting photometric redshifts to <em>HST</em> data only. One of these candidates appears as a spatially resolved arc. Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjlaadc10f1\">1</a> shows image cutouts of this candidate, hereafter SPT0615-JD1 (\"JD\" for <em>HST</em> F125W <em>J</em>-band dropout). The initial SExtractor segmentation map did not cover the entirety of the arc, so we additionally performed aperture photometry on the <em>HST</em> images of this candidate (elliptical aperture <em>a</em>\u00a0=\u00a01<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/farcs.gif\" alt=\"farcs\" align=\"bottom\" />8, <em>b</em>\u00a0=\u00a00<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/farcs.gif\" alt=\"farcs\" align=\"bottom\" />35, at an angle <em>\u03b8</em>\u00a0=\u00a0135\u00b0), and applied a local background subtraction. As described in Table <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"tabref\" href=\"#apjlaadc10t1\">1</a>, SPT0615-JD1 has an AB magnitude of 24.6\u00a0\u00b1\u00a00.1 in F160W detected with a single-to-noise ratio (S/N)\u00a0~\u00a010 (the F160W exposures were in two epochs 44 days apart, each detecting the source with S/N\u00a0~\u00a07). The extended arc shape is consistent with the direction of the lensing shear expected from the cluster (see Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjlaadc10s3\">3</a>). The bands blueward of F140W yield nondetections with S/N\u00a0<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/lesssim.gif\" alt=\"lesssim\" align=\"baseline\" />\u00a01, and F140W and F160W are both detected (S/N\u00a0=\u00a0 5.0 and 10.1, respectively). Importantly, we emphasize that the observed-frame size of SPT0516-JD is large (~2<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/farcs.gif\" alt=\"farcs\" align=\"bottom\" />5 long), and can easily be spatially resolved by <em>JWST</em> (see Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjlaadc10s5\">5</a>).</p><figure xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" id=\"apjlaadc10f1\" tabindex=\"-1\" role=\"group\" class=\"boxout boxout-bdr-grey keyboard-focus-only\" data-toolbar-type=\"figure\" data-toolbar-link=\"apjlaadc10f1\" data-toolbar-img=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/2041-8205/864/1/L22/Full/apjlaadc10f1_lr.jpg\" data-toolbar-title=\"Figure 1.\"><figure><div class=\"panzoom-container\"><div class=\"panzoom-parent\" style=\"overflow: hidden; position: relative;\"><img class=\"panzoom\" alt=\"Figure 1.\" src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/2041-8205/864/1/L22/Full/apjlaadc10f1_lr.jpg\" style=\"transform: matrix(1, 0, 0, 1, 0, 0); backface-visibility: hidden; transform-origin: 50% 50% 0px; cursor: move; transition: transform 200ms ease-in-out 0s;\"></img></div><div class=\"buttons zoom-tools\"><button class=\"zoom-in\"><span class=\"icon-zoomin\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom In\n\t\t\t\t</button><button class=\"zoom-out\"><span class=\"icon-zoomout\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom Out\n\t\t\t\t</button><span class=\"mobile-block\"><button class=\"reset\"><span class=\"icon-loop\"></span>\n\t                     Reset image size\n\t\t\t\t\t</button></span></div></div><figcaption><div class=\"article-text figure-caption\"><p><strong>Figure 1.</strong>\u00a0 3<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/farcm.gif\" alt=\"farcm\" align=\"baseline\" />25\u00a0<b>&#x00d7;</b>\u00a03<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/farcm.gif\" alt=\"farcm\" align=\"baseline\" />25 color image of the <em>HST</em> RELICS cluster field SPT0615-57. The yellow circle marks the location of the <em>z</em>\u00a0~\u00a010 candidate SPT0615-JD1. The white lines and large red circles show the <em>z</em>\u00a0=\u00a010 critical curves and predicted locations of the yet undetected counter images from the Paterno-Mahler et al. The small green and magenta circles show the positions of the two sets of multiple images used in their primary lens model, and the blue circles mark a set used in a lower likelihood model. The violet lines mark the edge where WFC3/IR data is available. The expanded inset is a 3''\u00a0<b>&#x00d7;</b>\u00a03'' WFC3/IR RGB color image with the R channel as F160W, G as the sum of F125W and F140W, and B as F105W. The bottom row of insets are the ACS images followed by WFC3/IR images, all 3''\u00a0<b>&#x00d7;</b>\u00a03''\u00a0and 60 mas resolution. The candidate is nondetected in the bands blueward of F140W, indicating a strong spectral break. Bottom row: larger 8''\u00a0<b>&#x00d7;</b>\u00a08'' cutouts with ellipses marking the position of SPT0615-JD1. Bottom left, right, and middle: A weighted stack of all four WFC3/IR bands centered on SPT0615-JD1, and the <em>Spitzer</em> 3.6 and 4.5 <em>\u03bc</em>m images. The <em>Spitzer</em> flux from the nearby bright <em>z</em>\u00a0~\u00a03 galaxy crowds the <em>z</em>\u00a0~\u00a010 candidate, which appears otherwise faint.</p></div><p class=\"mb-05
